This service was designed to be fast and private, and &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/1.1.1.1/privacy/public-dns-resolver/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;does not retain&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; information that would identify who is making a request.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In 2020, Cloudflare &lt;a href=\&quot;https://blog.cloudflare.com/introducing-1-1-1-1-for-families\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;announced 1.1.1.1 for Families&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, designed to add a layer of protection to our existing 1.1.1.1 public resolver. The intent behind this product was to provide consumers, namely families, the ability to add a security and adult content filter to block unsuspecting users from accessing specific sites when browsing the Internet.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Today, we are officially announcing that any ISP and equipment manufacturer can use our DNS resolvers for free. Internet service, network, and hardware equipment providers can sign up and join this program to partner with Cloudflare to deliver a safer browsing experience that is easy to use, industry leading, and &lt;b&gt;at no cost to anyone&lt;/b&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Leading companies have already partnered with Cloudflare to deliver superior and customized offerings to protect their customers. By delivering this service in a place where the customer is familiar, you can help us make the Internet a safe place for all.Â &lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n            &lt;h2 id=\&quot;a-need-to-intentionally-focus-on-families\&quot;&gt;A need to intentionally focus on families&lt;/h2&gt;\n            &lt;a href=\&quot;#a-need-to-intentionally-focus-on-families\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5\&quot;&gt;\n              &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n            &lt;/a&gt;\n          &lt;/div&gt;\n          &lt;p&gt;COVID-19 presented new challenges beginning in 2020 as kids&amp;#39; online activity increased and the reliance on home networks was more present than ever before. Research shows around &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.pewresearch.org/internet/2020/07/28/childrens-engagement-with-digital-devices-screen-time/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;67% of adolescents&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; have access to a tablet, with ages as low as two years old accessing media content. While it is often impressive to watch the ease with which a young child can navigate a smartphone or tablet handed to them and pull up their favorite YouTube show, it becomes increasingly concerning that kids may unintentionally stumble onto harmful content in the process.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Our launch of 1.1.1.1 for Families in 2020 provided that peace of mind to users around the globe, and it continues to deliver those protections. Today, households can set up this service using our &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/1.1.1.1/setup/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;guide&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;. They can select the DNS resolver they want to use, focusing on just privacy, or include blocking security threats and adult content across their entire home network.&lt;/p&gt;\n            &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card \&quot;&gt;\n            \n            &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/49EwOARWEuv8TfdzKdWpkc/bf53e440a69ae924ec09d806586ef567/image3.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1458\&quot; height=\&quot;660\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n            \n            &lt;/figure&gt;&lt;p&gt;Although this service is available and free for anyone to use, there are still many users who browse online daily without protections in place. Setting up protection like this can feel daunting, and many users are at a loss on where to begin and/or how to configure this on their devices or home network. Today we are announcing a partnership that will make setup and configuration much easier for users.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n            &lt;h2 id=\&quot;partnering-to-extend-security-even-further\&quot;&gt;Partnering to extend security even furtherÂ &lt;/h2&gt;\n            &lt;a href=\&quot;#partnering-to-extend-security-even-further\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5\&quot;&gt;\n              &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n            &lt;/a&gt;\n          &lt;/div&gt;\n          &lt;p&gt;ISPs and network providers can use Cloudflareâs different resolver services to provide various offerings to their customers. Our existing partners have taken these offerings and built them into their platforms as an extension of the services that they are already providing to their customers. This built-in model allows for easy adoption and a consistent and comprehensive end customer journey. Each service is designed with a specific purpose in mind, outlined below:&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;b&gt;Our core privacy resolver (1.1.1.1)&lt;/b&gt; is designed for speed and privacy.Â  Additionally, DNS requests to our public resolver can be sent over a secure channel using &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/1.1.1.1/encryption/dns-over-https/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;DNS over HTTPS (DoH)&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; or &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/1.1.1.1/encryption/dns-over-tls/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;DNS over TLS (DoT)&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, significantly decreasing the odds of any unwanted spying or monster-in-the-middle attacks.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;b&gt;Our security resolver (1.1.1.2)&lt;/b&gt; has all the benefits of 1.1.1.1, with the additional benefit of protecting users from sites that contain malware, spam, botnet command and control attacks, or phishing threats.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;b&gt;Our family resolver (1.1.1.3)&lt;/b&gt; provides all the benefits of 1.1.1.2, with the additional benefit of blocking unwanted adult content from both direct site navigation, as well as locking public search engines to Safe Search only. This prevents anyone from unknowingly searching for something that might return an unwanted result.Â &lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n            &lt;h3 id=\&quot;premium-safety-customizations\&quot;&gt;Premium Safety &amp; CustomizationsÂ &lt;/h3&gt;\n            &lt;a href=\&quot;#premium-safety-customizations\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5\&quot;&gt;\n              &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n            &lt;/a&gt;\n          &lt;/div&gt;\n        &lt;p&gt;If users want even more flexibility than what our public DNS resolvers provide, Cloudflare also offers a Gateway product that allows customized filtering, reporting, logging, analytics, and scheduling. This service was designed to be fast and private, and &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/1.1.1.1/privacy/public-dns-resolver/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;does not retain&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; information that would identify who is making a request.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In 2020, Cloudflare &lt;a href=\&quot;https://blog.cloudflare.com/introducing-1-1-1-1-for-families\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;announced 1.1.1.1 for Families&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, designed to add a layer of protection to our existing 1.1.1.1 public resolver. The intent behind this product was to provide consumers, namely families, the ability to add a security and adult content filter to block unsuspecting users from accessing specific sites when browsing the Internet.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Today, we are officially announcing that any ISP and equipment manufacturer can use our DNS resolvers for free. This built-in model allows for easy adoption and a consistent and comprehensive end customer journey. Each service is designed with a specific purpose in mind, outlined below:&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;b&gt;Our core privacy resolver (1.1.1.1)&lt;/b&gt; is designed for speed and privacy.Â  Additionally, DNS requests to our public resolver can be sent over a secure channel using &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/1.1.1.1/encryption/dns-over-https/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;DNS over HTTPS (DoH)&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; or &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/1.1.1.1/encryption/dns-over-tls/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;DNS over TLS (DoT)&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, significantly decreasing the odds of any unwanted spying or monster-in-the-middle attacks.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;b&gt;Our security resolver (1.1.1.2)&lt;/b&gt; has all the benefits of 1.1.1.1, with the additional benefit of protecting users from sites that contain malware, spam, botnet command and control attacks, or phishing threats.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;b&gt;Our family resolver (1.1.1.3)&lt;/b&gt; provides all the benefits of 1.1.1.2, with the additional benefit of blocking unwanted adult content from both direct site navigation, as well as locking public search engines to Safe Search only. As an authoritative DNS provider, we are responsible for managing and serving all the DNS records for our clientsâ domains. This means we have an enormous responsibility to provide the best service possible, starting at the data plane. As such, we are constantly investing in our infrastructure to ensure the reliability and performance of our systems.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.cloudflare.com/learning/dns/what-is-dns/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;DNS&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; is often referred to as the phone book of the Internet, and is a key component of the Internet. If you have ever used a phone book, you know that they can become extremely large depending on the size of the physical area it covers. A &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.cloudflare.com/en-gb/learning/dns/glossary/dns-zone/#:~:text=What%20is%20a%20DNS%20zone%20file%3F\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;zone file&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; in DNS is no different from a phone book. It has a list of records that provide details about a domain, usually including critical information like what IP address(es) each hostname is associated with. For example:&lt;/p&gt;\n            &lt;pre class=\&quot;language-Rust\&quot;&gt;&lt;code class=\&quot;language-Rust\&quot;&gt;example.com      59 IN A 198.51.100.0\nblog.example.com 59 IN A 198.51.100.1\nask.example.com  59 IN A 198.51.100.2&lt;/pre&gt;&lt;/code&gt;\n            &lt;p&gt;It is not unusual for these zone files to reach millions of records in size, just for a single domain. The biggest single zone on Cloudflare holds roughly 4 million DNS records, but the vast majority of zones hold fewer than 100 DNS records. Given our scale according to W3Techs, you can imagine how much DNS data alone Cloudflare is responsible for. Given this volume of data, and all the complexities that come at that scale, there needs to be a very good reason to move it from one database cluster to another.Â &lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n            &lt;h2 id=\&quot;why-migrate\&quot;&gt;Why migrateÂ &lt;/h2&gt;\n            &lt;a href=\&quot;#why-migrate\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5\&quot;&gt;\n              &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n            &lt;/a&gt;\n          &lt;/div&gt;\n          &lt;p&gt;When initially measured in 2022, DNS data took up approximately 40% of the storage capacity in Cloudflareâs main database cluster (&lt;b&gt;cfdb&lt;/b&gt;). This database cluster, consisting of a primary system and multiple replicas, is responsible for storing DNS zones, propagated to our &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.cloudflare.com/network/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;data centers in over 330 cities&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; via our distributed KV store &lt;a href=\&quot;https://blog.cloudflare.com/introducing-quicksilver-configuration-distribution-at-internet-scale/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Quicksilver&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;. &lt;b&gt;cfdb&lt;/b&gt; is accessed by most of Cloudflare&amp;#39;s APIs, including the &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/dns/manage-dns-records/how-to/create-dns-records/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;DNS Records API&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;. Today, the DNS Records API is the API most used by our customers, with each request resulting in a query to the database. As such, itâs always been important to optimize the DNS Records API and its surrounding infrastructure to ensure we can successfully serve every request that comes in.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;As Cloudflare scaled, &lt;b&gt;cfdb&lt;/b&gt; was becoming increasingly strained under the pressures of several services, many unrelated to DNS. During spikes of requests to our DNS systems, other Cloudflare services experienced degradation in the database performance. It was understood that in order to properly scale, we needed to optimize our database access and improve the systems that interact with it. However, it was evident that system level improvements could only be just so useful, and the growing pains were becoming unbearable. In late 2022, the DNS team decided, along with the help of 25 other teams, to detach itself from &lt;b&gt;cfdb&lt;/b&gt; and move our DNS records data to another database cluster.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n            &lt;h2 id=\&quot;pre-migration\&quot;&gt;Pre-migration&lt;/h2&gt;\n            &lt;a href=\&quot;#pre-migration\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5\&quot;&gt;\n              &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n            &lt;/a&gt;\n          &lt;/div&gt;\n          &lt;p&gt;From a DNS perspective, this migration to an improved database cluster was in the works for several years. Cloudflare initially relied on a single &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.postgresql.org/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Postgres&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; database cluster, &lt;b&gt;cfdb&lt;/b&gt;. At Cloudflare&amp;#39;s inception, &lt;b&gt;cfdb&lt;/b&gt; was responsible for storing information about zones and accounts and the majority of services on the Cloudflare control plane depended on it. Since around 2017, as Cloudflare grew, many services moved their data out of &lt;b&gt;cfdb&lt;/b&gt; to be served by a &lt;a href=\&quot;https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Microservices\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;microservice&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;. Unfortunately, the difficulty of these migrations are directly proportional to the amount of services that depend on the data being migrated, and in this case, most services require knowledge of both zones and DNS records.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Although the term âzoneâ was born from the DNS point of view, it has since evolved into something more. Today, zones on Cloudflare store many different types of non-DNS related settings and help link several non-DNS related products to customers&amp;#39; websites. Therefore, it didnât make sense to move both zone data and DNS record data together. This separation of two historically tightly coupled DNS concepts proved to be an incredibly challenging problem, involving many engineers and systems. In addition, it was clear that if we were going to dedicate the resources to solving this problem, we should also remove some of the legacy issues that came along with the original solution.Â &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;One of the main issues with the legacy database was that the DNS team had little control over which systems accessed exactly what data and at what rate. Moving to a new database gave us the opportunity to create a more tightly controlled interface to the DNS data. This was manifested as an internal DNS Records &lt;a href=\&quot;https://blog.cloudflare.com/moving-k8s-communication-to-grpc/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;gRPC API&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; which allows us to make sweeping changes to our data while only requiring a single change to the API, rather than coordinating with other systems.Â  For example, the DNS team can alter access logic and auditing procedures under the hood. In addition, it allows us to appropriately rate-limit and cache data depending on our needs. The move to this new API itself was no small feat, and with the help of several teams, we managed to migrate over 20 services, using 5 different programming languages, from direct database access to using our managed gRPC API. Many of these services touch very important areas such as &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/dns/dnssec/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;DNSSEC&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/ssl/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;TLS&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/email-routing/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Email&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/cloudflare-one/connections/connect-networks/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Tunnels&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/workers/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Workers&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/spectrum/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Spectrum&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, and &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/r2/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;R2 storage&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;. Therefore, it was important to get it right.Â &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;One of the last issues to tackle was the logical decoupling of common DNS database functions from zone data. Many of these functions expect to be able to access both DNS record data and DNS zone data at the same time. For example, at record creation time, our API needs to check that the zone is not over its maximum record allowance. Originally this check occurred at the SQL level by verifying that the record count was lower than the record limit for the zone. However, once you remove access to the zone itself, you are no longer able to confirm this. Our DNS Records API also made use of SQL functions to audit record changes, which requires access to both DNS record and zone data. Luckily, over the past several years, we have migrated this functionality out of our monolithic API and into separate microservices. This allowed us to move the auditing and zone setting logic to the application level rather than the database level. Ultimately, we are still taking advantage of SQL functions in the new database cluster, but they are fully independent of any other legacy systems, and are able to take advantage of the latest Postgres version.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Now that Cloudflare DNS was mostly decoupled from the zones database, it was time to proceed with the data migration. For this, we built what would become our &lt;b&gt;Change Data Capture and Transfer Service (CDCTS).&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n            &lt;h2 id=\&quot;requirements-for-the-change-data-capture-and-transfer-service\&quot;&gt;Requirements for the Change Data Capture and Transfer Service&lt;/h2&gt;\n            &lt;a href=\&quot;#requirements-for-the-change-data-capture-and-transfer-service\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5\&quot;&gt;\n              &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n            &lt;/a&gt;\n          &lt;/div&gt;\n          &lt;p&gt;The Database team is responsible for all Postgres clusters within Cloudflare, and were tasked with executing the data migration of two tables that store DNS data: &lt;i&gt;cf_rec&lt;/i&gt; and &lt;i&gt;cf_archived_rec&lt;/i&gt;, from the original &lt;b&gt;cfdb &lt;/b&gt;cluster to a new cluster we called &lt;b&gt;dnsdb&lt;/b&gt;.Â  We had several key requirements that drove our design:&lt;/p&gt;&lt;ul&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;b&gt;Donât lose data. &lt;/b&gt;This is the number one priority when handling any sort of data. Losing data means losing trust, and it is incredibly difficult to regain that trust once itâs lost.Â  Important in this is the ability to prove no data had been lost.Â  The migration process would, ideally, be easily auditable.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;b&gt;Minimize downtime&lt;/b&gt;.Â  We wanted a solution with less than a minute of downtime during the migration, and ideally with just a few seconds of delay.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ul&gt;&lt;p&gt;These two requirements meant that we had to be able to migrate data changes in near real-time, meaning we either needed to implement logical replication, or some custom method to capture changes, migrate them, and apply them in a table in a separate Postgres cluster.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;We first looked at using Postgres logical replication using &lt;a href=\&quot;https://github.com/2ndQuadrant/pglogical\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;pgLogical&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, but had concerns about its performance and our ability to audit its correctness.Â  Then some additional requirements emerged that made a pgLogical implementation of logical replication impossible:&lt;/p&gt;&lt;ul&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;b&gt;The ability to move data must be bidirectional.&lt;/b&gt; We had to have the ability to switch back to &lt;b&gt;cfdb&lt;/b&gt; without significant downtime in case of unforeseen problems with the new implementation.Â &lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;b&gt;Partition the &lt;/b&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;i&gt;cf_rec&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/b&gt;&lt;b&gt; table in the new database.&lt;/b&gt; This was a long-desired improvement and since most access to &lt;i&gt;cf_rec&lt;/i&gt; is by zone_id, it was decided that &lt;b&gt;mod(zone_id, num_partitions)&lt;/b&gt; would be the partition key.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;b&gt;Transferred data accessible from original database.Â  &lt;/b&gt;In case we had functionality that still needed access to data, a foreign table pointing to &lt;b&gt;dnsdb&lt;/b&gt; would be available in &lt;b&gt;cfdb&lt;/b&gt;. This could be used as emergency access to avoid needing to roll back the entire migration for a single missed process.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;b&gt;Only allow writes in one database. &lt;/b&gt;Â Applications should know where the primary database is, and should be blocked from writing to both databases at the same time.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ul&gt;\n          &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n            &lt;h2 id=\&quot;details-about-the-tables-being-migrated\&quot;&gt;Details about the tables being migrated&lt;/h2&gt;\n            &lt;a href=\&quot;#details-about-the-tables-being-migrated\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5\&quot;&gt;\n              &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n            &lt;/a&gt;\n          &lt;/div&gt;\n          &lt;p&gt;The primary table, &lt;i&gt;cf_rec&lt;/i&gt;, stores DNS record information, and its rows are regularly inserted, updated, and deleted. At the time of the migration, this table had 1.7 billion records, and with several indexes took up 1.5 TB of disk. Typical daily usage would observe 3-5 million inserts, 1 million updates, and 3-5 million deletes.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The second table, &lt;i&gt;cf_archived_rec&lt;/i&gt;, stores copies of &lt;i&gt;cf_rec&lt;/i&gt; that are obsolete â this table generally only has records inserted and is never updated or deleted.Â  As such, it would see roughly 3-5 million inserts per day, corresponding to the records deleted from &lt;i&gt;cf_rec&lt;/i&gt;. At the time of the migration, this table had roughly 4.3 billion records.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Fortunately, neither table made use of database triggers or foreign keys, which meant that we could insert/update/delete records in this table without triggering changes or worrying about dependencies on other tables.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Ultimately, both of these tables are highly active and are the source of truth for many highly critical systems at Cloudflare.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n            &lt;h2 id=\&quot;designing-the-change-data-capture-and-transfer-service\&quot;&gt;Designing the Change Data Capture and Transfer Service&lt;/h2&gt;\n            &lt;a href=\&quot;#designing-the-change-data-capture-and-transfer-service\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5\&quot;&gt;\n              &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n            &lt;/a&gt;\n          &lt;/div&gt;\n          &lt;p&gt;There were two main parts to this database migration:&lt;/p&gt;&lt;ol&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;b&gt;Initial copy:&lt;/b&gt; Take all the data from &lt;b&gt;cfdb &lt;/b&gt;and put it in &lt;b&gt;dnsdb.&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;b&gt;Change copy:&lt;/b&gt; Take all the changes in &lt;b&gt;cfdb &lt;/b&gt;since the initial copy and update &lt;b&gt;dnsdb&lt;/b&gt; to reflect them. This is the more involved part of the process.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ol&gt;&lt;p&gt;Normally, logical replication replays every insert, update, and delete on a copy of the data in the same transaction order, making a single-threaded pipeline.Â  We considered using a queue-based system but again, speed and auditability were both concerns as any queue would typically replay one change at a time.Â  We wanted to be able to apply large sets of changes, so that after an initial dump and restore, we could quickly catch up with the changed data. For the rest of the blog, we will only speak about &lt;i&gt;cf_rec&lt;/i&gt; for simplicity, but the process for &lt;i&gt;cf_archived_rec&lt;/i&gt; is the same.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;What we decided on was a simple change capture table. Rows from this capture table would be loaded in real-time by a database trigger, with a transfer service that could migrate and apply thousands of changed records to &lt;b&gt;dnsdb&lt;/b&gt; in each batch. Lastly, we added some auditing logic on top to ensure that we could easily verify that all data was safely transferred without downtime.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n            &lt;h3 id=\&quot;basic-model-of-change-data-capture\&quot;&gt;Basic model of change data captureÂ &lt;/h3&gt;\n            &lt;a href=\&quot;#basic-model-of-change-data-capture\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5\&quot;&gt;\n              &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n            &lt;/a&gt;\n          &lt;/div&gt;\n        &lt;p&gt;For &lt;i&gt;cf_rec&lt;/i&gt; to be migrated, we would create a change logging table, along with a trigger function and aÂ  table trigger to capture the new state of the record after any insert/update/delete.Â Â &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The change logging table named &lt;i&gt;log_cf_rec&lt;/i&gt; had the same columns as &lt;i&gt;cf_rec&lt;/i&gt;, as well as four new columns:&lt;/p&gt;&lt;ul&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;b&gt;change_id&lt;/b&gt;:Â  a sequence generated unique identifier of the record&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;b&gt;action&lt;/b&gt;: a single character indicating whether this record represents an [i]nsert, [u]pdate, or [d]elete&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;b&gt;change_timestamp&lt;/b&gt;: the date/time when the change record was created&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;b&gt;change_user:&lt;/b&gt; the database user that made the change.Â Â &lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ul&gt;&lt;p&gt;A trigger was placed on the &lt;i&gt;cf_rec&lt;/i&gt; table so that each insert/update would copy the new values of the record into the change table, and for deletes, create a &amp;#39;D&amp;#39; record with the primary key value.Â &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Here is an example of the change logging where we delete, re-insert, update, and finally select from the &lt;i&gt;log_cf_rec&lt;/i&gt;&lt;b&gt; &lt;/b&gt;table. Note that the actual &lt;i&gt;cf_rec&lt;/i&gt; and &lt;i&gt;log_cf_rec&lt;/i&gt; tables have many more columns, but have been edited for simplicity.&lt;/p&gt;\n            &lt;pre class=\&quot;language-Rust\&quot;&gt;&lt;code class=\&quot;language-Rust\&quot;&gt;dns_records=# DELETE FROM  cf_rec WHERE rec_id = 13;\n\ndns_records=# SELECT * from log_cf_rec;\nChange_id | action | rec_id | zone_id | name\n----------------------------------------------\n1         | D      | 13     |         |   \n\ndns_records=# INSERT INTO cf_rec VALUES(13,299,&amp;#039;cloudflare.example.com&amp;#039;);  \n\ndns_records=# UPDATE cf_rec SET name = &amp;#039;test.example.com&amp;#039; WHERE rec_id = 13;\n\ndns_records=# SELECT * from log_cf_rec;\nChange_id | action | rec_id | zone_id | name\n----------------------------------------------\n1         | D      | 13     |         |  \n2         | I      | 13     | 299     | cloudflare.example.com\n3         | U      | 13     | 299     | test.example.com &lt;/pre&gt;&lt;/code&gt;\n            &lt;p&gt;In addition to &lt;i&gt;log_cf_rec&lt;/i&gt;, we also introduced 2 more tables in &lt;b&gt;cfdb &lt;/b&gt;and 3 more tables in &lt;b&gt;dnsdb:&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;b&gt;cfdb&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;ol&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;i&gt;transferred_log_cf_rec&lt;/i&gt;: Responsible for auditing the batches transferred to &lt;b&gt;dnsdb&lt;/b&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;i&gt;log_change_action&lt;/i&gt;:&lt;i&gt; &lt;/i&gt;Responsible for summarizing the transfer size in order to compare with the &lt;i&gt;log_change_action &lt;/i&gt;in &lt;b&gt;dnsdb.&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ol&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;b&gt;dnsdb&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;ol&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;i&gt;migrate_log_cf_rec&lt;/i&gt;:&lt;i&gt; &lt;/i&gt;Responsible for collecting batch changes in &lt;b&gt;dnsdb&lt;/b&gt;, which would later be applied to &lt;i&gt;cf_rec &lt;/i&gt;in &lt;b&gt;dnsdb&lt;/b&gt;&lt;i&gt;.&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;i&gt;applied_migrate_log_cf_rec&lt;/i&gt;:&lt;i&gt; &lt;/i&gt;Responsible for auditing the batches that had been successfully applied to cf_rec in &lt;b&gt;dnsdb.&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;i&gt;log_change_action&lt;/i&gt;:&lt;i&gt; &lt;/i&gt;Responsible for summarizing the transfer size in order to compare with the &lt;i&gt;log_change_action &lt;/i&gt;in &lt;b&gt;cfdb.&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ol&gt;\n          &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n            &lt;h3 id=\&quot;initial-copy\&quot;&gt;Initial copy&lt;/h3&gt;\n            &lt;a href=\&quot;#initial-copy\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5\&quot;&gt;\n              &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n            &lt;/a&gt;\n          &lt;/div&gt;\n        &lt;p&gt;With change logging in place, we were now ready to do the initial copy of the tables from &lt;b&gt;cfdb&lt;/b&gt; to &lt;b&gt;dnsdb&lt;/b&gt;. Because we were changing the structure of the tables in the destination database and because of network timeouts, we wanted to bring the data over in small pieces and validate that it was brought over accurately, rather than doing a single multi-hour copy or &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.postgresql.org/docs/current/app-pgdump.html\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;pg_dump&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;.Â  We also wanted to ensure a long-running read could not impact production and that the process could be paused and resumed at any time.Â  The basic model to transfer data was done with a simple psql copy statement piped into another psql copy statement.Â  No intermediate files were used.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;code&gt;psql_cfdb -c &amp;quot;COPY (SELECT * FROM cf_rec WHERE id BETWEEN n and n+1000000 TO STDOUT)&amp;quot; |Â &lt;/code&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;code&gt;psql_dnsdb -c &amp;quot;COPY cf_rec FROM STDIN&amp;quot;&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Prior to a batch being moved, the count of records to be moved was recorded in &lt;b&gt;cfdb&lt;/b&gt;, and after each batch was moved, a count was recorded in &lt;b&gt;dnsdb&lt;/b&gt; and compared to the count in &lt;b&gt;cfdb&lt;/b&gt; to ensure that a network interruption or other unforeseen error did not cause data to be lost. The bash script to copy data looked like this, where we included files that could be touched to pause or end the copy (if they cause load on production or there was an incident).Â  Once again, this code below has been heavily simplified.&lt;/p&gt;\n            &lt;pre class=\&quot;language-Python\&quot;&gt;&lt;code class=\&quot;language-Python\&quot;&gt;#!/bin/bash\nfor i in &amp;quot;$@&amp;quot;; do\n   # Allow user to control whether this is paused or not via pause_copy file\n   while [ -f pause_copy ]; do\n      sleep 1\n   done\n   # Allow user to end migration by creating end_copy file\n   if [ ! -f end_copy ]; then\n      # Copy a batch of records from cfdb to dnsdb\n      # Get count of records from cfdb \n\t# Get count of records from dnsdb\n \t# Compare cfdb count with dnsdb count and alert if different \n   fi\ndone\n&lt;/pre&gt;&lt;/code&gt;\n            &lt;p&gt;&lt;sup&gt;&lt;i&gt;Bash copy script&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n            &lt;h3 id=\&quot;change-copy\&quot;&gt;Change copy&lt;/h3&gt;\n            &lt;a href=\&quot;#change-copy\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5\&quot;&gt;\n              &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n            &lt;/a&gt;\n          &lt;/div&gt;\n        &lt;p&gt;Once the initial copy was completed, we needed to update &lt;b&gt;dnsdb&lt;/b&gt; with any changes that had occurred in &lt;b&gt;cfdb&lt;/b&gt; since the start of the initial copy. To implement this change copy, we created a function &lt;i&gt;fn_log_change_transfer_log_cf_rec &lt;/i&gt;that could be passed a &lt;i&gt;batch_id&lt;/i&gt; and &lt;i&gt;batch_size&lt;/i&gt;, and did 5 things, all of which were executed in a single database &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.postgresql.org/docs/current/tutorial-transactions.html\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;transaction&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;:&lt;/p&gt;&lt;ol&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Select a &lt;i&gt;batch_size&lt;/i&gt; of records from &lt;i&gt;log_cf_rec&lt;/i&gt; in &lt;b&gt;cfdb&lt;/b&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Copy the batch to &lt;i&gt;transferred_log_cf_rec&lt;/i&gt; in &lt;b&gt;cfdb &lt;/b&gt;to mark it as transferred.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Delete the batch from &lt;i&gt;log_cf_rec&lt;/i&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Write a summary of the action to &lt;i&gt;log_change_action&lt;/i&gt; table. This will later be used to compare transferred records with &lt;b&gt;cfdb&lt;/b&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Return the batch of records.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ol&gt;&lt;p&gt;We then took the returned batch of records and copied them to &lt;i&gt;migrate_log_cf_rec &lt;/i&gt;in &lt;b&gt;dnsdb&lt;/b&gt;. We used the same bash script as above, except this time, the copy command looked like this:&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;code&gt;psql_cfdb -c &amp;quot;COPY (SELECT * FROM &lt;/code&gt;&lt;code&gt;&lt;i&gt;fn_log_change_transfer_log_cf_rec(&amp;lt;batch_id&amp;gt;,&amp;lt;batch_size&amp;gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/code&gt;&lt;code&gt;) TO STDOUT&amp;quot; |Â &lt;/code&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;code&gt;psql_dnsdb -c &amp;quot;COPY migrate_log_cf_rec FROM STDIN&amp;quot;&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n            &lt;h3 id=\&quot;applying-changes-in-the-destination-database\&quot;&gt;Applying changes in the destination database&lt;/h3&gt;\n            &lt;a href=\&quot;#applying-changes-in-the-destination-database\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5\&quot;&gt;\n              &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n            &lt;/a&gt;\n          &lt;/div&gt;\n        &lt;p&gt;Now, with a batch of data in the &lt;i&gt;migrate_log_cf_rec &lt;/i&gt;table, we called a newly created function &lt;i&gt;log_change_apply&lt;/i&gt; to apply and audit the changes. Once again, this was all executed within a single database transaction. The function did the following:&lt;/p&gt;&lt;ol&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Move a batch from the &lt;i&gt;migrate_log_cf_rec&lt;/i&gt; table to a new temporary table.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Write the counts for the batch_id to the &lt;i&gt;log_change_action&lt;/i&gt; table.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Delete from the temporary table all but the latest record for a unique id (last action). For example, an insert followed by 30 updates would have a single record left, the final update. There is no need to apply all the intermediate updates.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Delete any record from &lt;i&gt;cf_rec&lt;/i&gt; that has any corresponding changes.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Insert any [i]nsert or [u]pdate records in &lt;i&gt;cf_rec&lt;/i&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Copy the batch to &lt;i&gt;applied_migrate_log_cf_rec&lt;/i&gt; for a full audit trail.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ol&gt;\n          &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n            &lt;h3 id=\&quot;putting-it-all-together\&quot;&gt;Putting it all together&lt;/h3&gt;\n            &lt;a href=\&quot;#putting-it-all-together\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5\&quot;&gt;\n              &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n            &lt;/a&gt;\n          &lt;/div&gt;\n        &lt;p&gt;There were 4 distinct phases, each of which was part of a different database transaction:&lt;/p&gt;&lt;ol&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Call &lt;i&gt;fn_log_change_transfer_log_cf_rec &lt;/i&gt;in &lt;b&gt;cfdb &lt;/b&gt;to get a batch of records.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Copy the batch of records to &lt;b&gt;dnsdb.&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Call &lt;i&gt;log_change_apply &lt;/i&gt;in &lt;b&gt;dnsdb &lt;/b&gt;to apply the batch of records.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Compare the &lt;i&gt;log_change_action&lt;/i&gt; table in each respective database to ensure counts match.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ol&gt;\n          &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card\&quot;&gt;\n          &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/2REIq71tc7M4jKPLZSJzS9/11f22f700300f2ad3a5ee5ca85a75480/Applying_changes_in_the_destination_database.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;3406\&quot; height=\&quot;1873\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n          &lt;/figure&gt;&lt;p&gt;This process was run every 3 seconds for several weeks before the migration to ensure that we could keep &lt;b&gt;dnsdb&lt;/b&gt; in sync with &lt;b&gt;cfdb&lt;/b&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n            &lt;h2 id=\&quot;managing-which-database-is-live\&quot;&gt;Managing which database is live&lt;/h2&gt;\n            &lt;a href=\&quot;#managing-which-database-is-live\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5\&quot;&gt;\n              &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n            &lt;/a&gt;\n          &lt;/div&gt;\n          &lt;p&gt;The last major pre-migration task was the construction of the request locking system that would be used throughout the actual migration. The aim was to create a system that would allow the database to communicate with the DNS Records API, to allow the DNS Records API to handle HTTP connections more gracefully. If done correctly, this could reduce downtime for DNS Record API users to nearly zero.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In order to facilitate this, a new table called &lt;i&gt;cf_migration_manager&lt;/i&gt; was created. The table would be periodically polled by the DNS Records API, communicating two critical pieces of information:&lt;/p&gt;&lt;ol&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;b&gt;Which database was active.&lt;/b&gt; Here we just used a simple A or B naming convention.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;b&gt;If the database was locked for writing&lt;/b&gt;. In the event the database was locked for writing, the DNS Records API would hold HTTP requests until the lock was released by the database.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ol&gt;&lt;p&gt;Both pieces of information would be controlled within a migration manager script.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The benefit of migrating the 20+ internal services from direct database access to using our internal DNS Records gRPC API is that we were able to control access to the database to ensure that no one else would be writing without going through the &lt;i&gt;cf_migration_manager&lt;/i&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n            &lt;h2 id=\&quot;during-the-migration\&quot;&gt;During the migrationÂ &lt;/h2&gt;\n            &lt;a href=\&quot;#during-the-migration\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5\&quot;&gt;\n              &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n            &lt;/a&gt;\n          &lt;/div&gt;\n          &lt;p&gt;Although we aimed to complete this migration in a matter of seconds, we announced a DNS maintenance window that could last a couple of hours just to be safe. Now that everything was set up, and both &lt;b&gt;cfdb&lt;/b&gt; and &lt;b&gt;dnsdb&lt;/b&gt; were roughly in sync, it was time to proceed with the migration. The steps were as follows:&lt;/p&gt;&lt;ol&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Lower the time between copies from 3s to 0.5s.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Lock &lt;b&gt;cfdb&lt;/b&gt; for writes via &lt;i&gt;cf_migration_manager&lt;/i&gt;. This would tell the DNS Records API to hold write connections.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Make &lt;b&gt;cfdb&lt;/b&gt; read-only and migrate the last logged changes to &lt;b&gt;dnsdb&lt;/b&gt;.Â &lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Enable writes to &lt;b&gt;dnsdb&lt;/b&gt;.Â &lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Tell DNS Records API that &lt;b&gt;dnsdb&lt;/b&gt; is the new primary database and that write connections can proceed via the &lt;i&gt;cf_migration_manager&lt;/i&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ol&gt;&lt;p&gt;Since we needed to ensure that the last changes were copied to &lt;b&gt;dnsdb&lt;/b&gt; before enabling writing, this entire process took no more than 2 seconds. During the migration we saw a spike of API latency as a result of the migration manager locking writes, and then dealing with a backlog of queries. However, we recovered back to normal latencies after several minutes.Â &lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card\&quot;&gt;\n          &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/6agUpD8BQVxgDupBrwtTw3/38c96f91879c6539011866821ad6f11a/image3.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1230\&quot; height=\&quot;503\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n          &lt;/figure&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;sup&gt;&lt;i&gt;DNS Records API Latency and Requests during migration&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Unfortunately, due to the far-reaching impact that DNS has at Cloudflare, this was not the end of the migration. There were 3 lesser-used services that had slipped by in our scan of services accessing DNS records via &lt;b&gt;cfdb&lt;/b&gt;. Fortunately, the setup of the foreign table meant that we could very quickly fix any residual issues by simply changing the table name.Â &lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n            &lt;h2 id=\&quot;post-migration\&quot;&gt;Post-migration&lt;/h2&gt;\n            &lt;a href=\&quot;#post-migration\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5\&quot;&gt;\n              &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n            &lt;/a&gt;\n          &lt;/div&gt;\n          &lt;p&gt;Almost immediately, as expected, we saw a steep drop in usage across &lt;b&gt;cfdb&lt;/b&gt;. This freed up a lot of resources for other services to take advantage of.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card\&quot;&gt;\n          &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/Xfnbc9MZLwJB91ypItWsi/1eb21362893b31a1e3c846d1076a9f5b/image6.jpg\&quot; alt=\&quot;\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1999\&quot; height=\&quot;909\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n          &lt;/figure&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;sup&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;b&gt;cfdb&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;sup&gt;&lt;i&gt; usage dropped significantly after the migration period.&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Since the migration, the average &lt;b&gt;requests&lt;/b&gt; per second to the DNS Records API has more than &lt;b&gt;doubled&lt;/b&gt;. At the same time, our CPU usage across both &lt;b&gt;cfdb&lt;/b&gt; and &lt;b&gt;dnsdb&lt;/b&gt; has settled at below 10% as seen below, giving us room for spikes and future growth.Â &lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card\&quot;&gt;\n          &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/39su35dkb5Pl8uwYfYjHLg/0eb26ced30b44efb71abb73830e01f3a/image2.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1550\&quot; height=\&quot;672\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n          &lt;/figure&gt;\n          &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card\&quot;&gt;\n          &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/5AdlLKXtD68QWCsMVLKnkt/9137beee9c941827eb57c53825ffe209/image4.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1568\&quot; height=\&quot;678\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n          &lt;/figure&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;sup&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;b&gt;cfdb&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;sup&gt;&lt;i&gt; and &lt;/i&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;sup&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;b&gt;dnsdb&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;sup&gt;&lt;i&gt; CPU usage now&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;As a result of this improved capacity, our database-related incident rate dropped dramatically.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;As for query latencies, our latency post-migration is slightly lower on average, with fewer sustained spikes above 500ms. However, the performance improvement is largely noticed during high load periods, when our database handles spikes without significant issues. Many of these spikes come as a result of clients making calls to collect a large amount of DNS records or making several changes to their zone in short bursts. Both of these actions are common use cases for large customers onboarding zones.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In addition to these improvements, the DNS team also has more granular control over &lt;b&gt;dnsdb&lt;/b&gt; cluster-specific settings that can be tweaked for our needs rather than catering to all the other services. For example, we were able to make custom changes to replication lag limits to ensure that services using replicas were able to read with some amount of certainty that the data would exist in a consistent form. Measures like this reduce overall load on the primary because almost all read queries can now go to the replicas.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Although this migration was a resounding success, we are always working to improve our systems. As we grow, so do our customers, which means the need to scale never really ends. We have more exciting improvements on the roadmap, and we are looking forward to sharing more details in the future.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The DNS team at Cloudflare isnât the only team solving challenging problems like the one above. Okta&quot;],&quot;slug&quot;:[0,&quot;protect-against-identity-based-attacks-by-sharing-cloudflare-user-risk-with-okta&quot;],&quot;excerpt&quot;:[0,&quot;Uphold Zero Trust principles and protect against identity-based attacks by sharing Cloudflare user risk scores with Okta. Learn how this new integration allows your organization to mitigate risk in real time, make informed access decisions, and free up security resources with automation.&quot;],&quot;featured&quot;:[0,false],&quot;html&quot;:[0,&quot;&lt;p&gt;Cloudflare One, our &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.cloudflare.com/learning/access-management/what-is-sase/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;secure access service edge (SASE)&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; platform, is introducing a new integration with Okta, the &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.cloudflare.com/learning/access-management/what-is-identity-and-access-management/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;identity and access management (IAM)&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; vendor, to share risk indicators in real-time and simplify how organizations can dynamically manage their security posture in response to changes across their environments.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For many organizations, it is becoming increasingly challenging and inefficient to adapt to risks across their growing &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.cloudflare.com/learning/security/what-is-an-attack-surface/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;attack surface&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;. In particular, security teams struggle with multiple siloed tools that fail to share risk data effectively with each other, leading to excessive manual effort to extract signals from the noise. To address this complexity, Cloudflare launched &lt;a href=\&quot;https://blog.cloudflare.com/unified-risk-posture/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;risk posture management capabilities&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; earlier this year to make it easier for organizations to accomplish three key jobs on one platform: &lt;/p&gt;&lt;ol&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Evaluating risk posed by people by using first-party &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.cloudflare.com/learning/security/what-is-ueba/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;user entity and behavior analytics (UEBA)&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; models&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Exchanging risk telemetry with best-in-class security tools, and&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Enforcing risk controls based on those dynamic first- and third-party risk scores.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ol&gt;&lt;p&gt;Todayâs announcement builds on these capabilities (particularly job #2) and &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.cloudflare.com/partners/technology-partners/okta/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;our partnership with Okta&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; by enabling organizations to share Cloudflareâs real-time &lt;a href=\&quot;https://blog.cloudflare.com/cf1-user-risk-score/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;user risk scores&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; with Okta, which can then automatically enforce policies based on that userâs risk. In this way, organizations can adapt to evolving risks in less time with less manual effort.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n            &lt;h2 id=\&quot;cloudflares-user-risk-scoring\&quot;&gt;Cloudflareâs user risk scoring&lt;/h2&gt;\n            &lt;a href=\&quot;#cloudflares-user-risk-scoring\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5\&quot;&gt;\n              &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n            &lt;/a&gt;\n          &lt;/div&gt;\n          &lt;p&gt;&lt;a href=\&quot;https://blog.cloudflare.com/cf1-user-risk-score/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Introduced earlier this year&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, Cloudflareâs user risk scoring analyzes real-time telemetry of user activities and behaviors and assigns a risk score of high, medium, or low. For example, if Cloudflare detects risky or suspicious activity from a user â such as impossible travel, where a user logs in from multiple geographically dispersed locations within a short time frame, data loss prevention (DLP) detections, or endpoint detections suggesting that the device is infected â the userâs risk score will increase. The activity leading to that scoring is logged for analysis.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Cloudflare includes &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/cloudflare-one/insights/risk-score/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;predefined risk behaviors&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; to help you get started. Administrators can create policies based on specific risk behaviors and adjust the risk level for each behavior based on their companyâs tolerance.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n            &lt;h2 id=\&quot;share-risk-scores-with-okta-and-take-action-automatically\&quot;&gt;Share risk scores with Okta and take action automatically&lt;/h2&gt;\n            &lt;a href=\&quot;#share-risk-scores-with-okta-and-take-action-automatically\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5\&quot;&gt;\n              &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n            &lt;/a&gt;\n          &lt;/div&gt;\n          &lt;p&gt;Customers that opt in to this new integration will be able to share continually updated Cloudflare user risk scores with &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.okta.com/products/identity-threat-protection/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Identity Threat Protection with Okta AI&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;. If a user is deemed too risky, Okta will automatically take action to mitigate the risk, such as enforcing &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.cloudflare.com/en-gb/learning/access-management/what-is-multi-factor-authentication/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;multi-factor authentication (MFA)&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; verification or universally logging the user out from all applications.Â &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For example, a user has a low risk score from Cloudflare that was shared with Okta, but after exhibiting âimpossible travelâ behavior, the userâs risk level is raised to high. Cloudflare sends the updated score to Okta, which triggers a Universal Logout and an MFA challenge if the user attempts to log in again. Access to sensitive systems may be revoked completely until the user is verified.Â &lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n            &lt;h2 id=\&quot;how-it-works-continuous-risk-evaluation-and-exchange\&quot;&gt;How it works: continuous risk evaluation and exchange&lt;/h2&gt;\n            &lt;a href=\&quot;#how-it-works-continuous-risk-evaluation-and-exchange\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5\&quot;&gt;\n              &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n            &lt;/a&gt;\n          &lt;/div&gt;\n          \n          &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card\&quot;&gt;\n          &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/79JiNwP0P5bbXpW6dy6ORQ/b0dc91943840b44bbcc8e447af64f392/image1.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1999\&quot; height=\&quot;768\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n          &lt;/figure&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;sup&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;i&gt;Figure 1.&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;sup&gt;&lt;i&gt; Diagram showing risky behavior by a user, resulting in sign-out.&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;We begin by detecting risky behavior from a user (such as an âimpossible travelâ event between two geographic locations). Instances of risky behavior are called Risk Events. We perform two actions when we observe a Risk Event: logging the event and evaluating whether further action is required. For customers that have enabled &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/cloudflare-one/insights/risk-score/#send-risk-score-to-okta\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Risk Score Sharing with Okta&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, any change in Risk Score is transmitted to Oktaâs Identity Threat Protection (ITP).&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Upon receiving a new event, Okta evaluates the change in user risk against the organization&amp;#39;s policies. These policies may include actions such as re-authenticating the user if they become high risk.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;When we design new features, we aim for them to be extensible across the industry. For this reason, we chose the &lt;a href=\&quot;https://openid.net/specs/openid-sharedsignals-framework-1_0.html\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;OpenID Shared Signals Framework Specification (SSF)&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; to be the foundation of our transmission format. By doing this, we are able to leverage current and future providers that support the standard. The core functionality of SSF revolves around sharing &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc8417.html\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Security Event Tokens (SETs)&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, a specialized version of a JSON Web Token (JWT). Providers can produce and consume Security Event Tokens, forming a ânetworkâ of shared user risk information between providers.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card\&quot;&gt;\n          &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/SaWKy4UWPZfa8hf6rHcF8/571a08ddeab08b01b9a38e740ec89644/image2.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1999\&quot; height=\&quot;1000\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n          &lt;/figure&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;sup&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;i&gt;Figure 2.&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;sup&gt;&lt;i&gt; Diagram showing a Security Event Token being transmitted from Cloudflare to Okta.&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The diagram above (&lt;b&gt;Figure 2&lt;/b&gt;) details the process of sharing risk. When sharing Risk Score changes with Okta, we bundle metadata about the risk event and user into the body of a Security Event Token. Following this, the JWT/SET is signed using our private key. This is an important step, as the signature is used to verify the sender&amp;#39;s identity (cryptographic authenticity) and that the payload body has not been tampered with (cryptographic integrity). In plain terms, this signature is used by Okta to verify that the event is unaltered and was sent by Cloudflare.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Once Okta has verified the authenticity and integrity of the SET token, they may use the risk metadata within the body to execute Identity Threat Protection policies defined by the customer. These policies could include actions such as âif a high risk score is received from Cloudflare, sign out the offending userâ.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Learn more about the Shared Signals Framework and CAEP in &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.okta.com/blog/2024/08/identity-threat-protection-with-okta-ai/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Oktaâs announcement blog post&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n            &lt;h2 id=\&quot;get-started-today\&quot;&gt;Get started today&lt;/h2&gt;\n            &lt;a href=\&quot;#get-started-today\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5\&quot;&gt;\n              &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n            &lt;/a&gt;\n          &lt;/div&gt;\n          &lt;p&gt;Cloudflare customers can easily &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/cloudflare-one/insights/risk-score/#send-risk-score-to-okta\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;enable risk score sharing from the Cloudflare One SSO setup page&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;. This is available to customers whether youâve already integrated with Okta or are setting up the integration for the first time. You will also be able to confirm that the feature was enabled in your audit logs.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;If youâve already integrated Okta within your Cloudflare One dashboard:&lt;/p&gt;&lt;ol&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;As an admin, navigate to Settings &amp;gt; Authentication and select the Okta login method.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Select âsend risk score to Okta.â&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ol&gt;&lt;p&gt;If you havenât yet integrated Okta within your Cloudflare One dashboard:&lt;/p&gt;&lt;ol&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;As an admin, navigate to Settings &amp;gt; Authentication and select a new login method.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Follow the instructions to add Okta as an SSO.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Select âsend risk score to Okta.â&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ol&gt;&lt;p&gt;Now, whenever a userâs risk score changes within the organization, information is sent to Okta automatically and an audit log is documented.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n            &lt;h2 id=\&quot;uphold-zero-trust-principles\&quot;&gt;Uphold Zero Trust principles&lt;/h2&gt;\n            &lt;a href=\&quot;#uphold-zero-trust-principles\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n            &lt;/a&gt;\n          &lt;/div&gt;\n          &lt;p&gt;In conclusion, the ability to incorporate rich context is essential for making accurate and informed access decisions. With vast amounts of data â including user logins, logouts, websites visited, and emails sent â human analysts would struggle to keep pace with modern security challenges. Cloudflare provides context in the form of a risk score, enabling Oktaâs risk engine to make more informed policy decisions about users. This acquisition extends our SASE portfolio to incorporate inline cloud app controls, empowering &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.cloudflare.com/zero-trust/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Cloudflare One&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; customers with preventative security controls for all their cloud services.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In todayâs digital landscape, cloud services and SaaS (software as a service) apps have become indispensable for the daily operation of organizations. At the same time, the amount of data flowing between organizations and their cloud providers has ballooned, increasing the chances of data leakage, compliance issues, and worse, opportunities for attackers. Additionally, many companies â especially at enterprise scale â are working directly with multiple cloud providers for flexibility based on the strengths, resiliency against outages or errors, and cost efficiencies of different clouds.Â &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Security teams that rely on &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.cloudflare.com/learning/cloud/what-is-cspm/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Cloud Security Posture Management (CSPM)&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; or similar tools for monitoring cloud configurations and permissions and Infrastructure as code (IaC) scanning are falling short due to detecting issues only after misconfigurations occur with an overwhelming volume of alerts. The combination of Kivera and Cloudflare One puts preventive controls directly into the deployment process, or âinlineâ, blocking errors before they happen. This offers a proactive approach essential to protecting cloud infrastructure from evolving cyber threats, maintaining data security, and accelerating compliance.Â &lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n            &lt;h2 id=\&quot;an-early-warning-system-for-cloud-security-risks\&quot;&gt;An early warning system for cloud security risksÂ &lt;/h2&gt;\n            &lt;a href=\&quot;#an-early-warning-system-for-cloud-security-risks\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5\&quot;&gt;\n              &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n            &lt;/a&gt;\n          &lt;/div&gt;\n          &lt;p&gt;In a significant leap forward in cloud security, the combination of Kiveraâs technology and Cloudflare One adds preventive, inline controls to enforce secure configurations for cloud resources. By inspecting cloud API traffic, these new capabilities equip organizations with enhanced visibility and granular controls, allowing for a proactive approach in mitigating risks, managing cloud security posture, and embracing a streamlined DevOps process when deploying cloud infrastructure.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Kivera will add the following capabilities to Cloudflareâs &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.cloudflare.com/learning/access-management/what-is-sase/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;SASE&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; platform:&lt;/p&gt;&lt;ul&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;b&gt;One-click security:&lt;/b&gt; Customers benefit from immediate prevention of the most common cloud breaches caused by misconfigurations, such as accidentally allowing public access or policy inconsistencies.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;b&gt;Enforced cloud tenant control:&lt;/b&gt; Companies can easily draw boundaries around their cloud resources and tenants to ensure that sensitive data stays within their organization.Â &lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;b&gt;Prevent data exfiltration:&lt;/b&gt; Easily set rules to prevent data being sent to unauthorized locations.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;b&gt;Reduce âshadowâ cloud infrastructure:&lt;/b&gt; Ensure that every interaction between a customer and their cloud provider is in line with preset standards.Â &lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;b&gt;Streamline cloud security compliance:&lt;/b&gt; Customers can automatically assess and enforce compliance against the most common regulatory frameworks.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;b&gt;Flexible DevOps model:&lt;/b&gt; Enforce bespoke controls independent of public cloud setup and deployment tools, minimizing the layers of lock-in between an organization and a cloud provider.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;b&gt;Complementing other cloud security tools:&lt;/b&gt; Create a first line of defense for cloud deployment errors, reducing the volume of alerts for customers also using CSPM tools or Cloud Native Application Protection Platforms (CNAPPs).Â &lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ul&gt;\n          &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card\&quot;&gt;\n          &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/7nALx5Qv8FBYxn1R6RkUvX/1b3dddb60d9d85142a9fda82d2eee381/BLOG-2592_2.png\&quot;
